Billings for 1995 were estimated at $25 million.   Richard Zien, a managing partner at Mendelsohn/Zien, said on Friday that his agency was awarded the account after a ``shootout'' with Grey. Mendelson/Zien had been one of three finalists in a previous Carl's Jr. review, he added, and had worked for the chain since October on a strategic assignment.   Volkswagen of America Inc. has narrowed to five finalists the review on its account with estimated billings of $90 million. Berlin Cameron Doyle in New York, which was dismissed in December, had handled the account since August 1993.   Maria Leonhauser, a spokeswoman for Volkswagen in Auburn Hills, Mich., identified the finalists on Friday as Arnold Fortuna Lawner &AMP; Cabot in Boston; DDB Needham New York, which handled the account before Berlin Cameron; Deutsch Inc. in New York; the Martin Agency in Richmond, and the Richards Group in Dallas. A decision is expected by the end of March, she added, when Berlin Cameron completes its work on the account.   Neil Kraft, a well-known creative executive in fashion and retail advertising, has joined Elizabeth Arden Inc., the cosmetics and fragrance company, as a consultant.   Susan Arnot, a spokeswoman for Arden in New York, said Friday that Kraft was consulting on a ``very open-ended basis'' on creative elements like ``the visual image of Arden,'' working with executives like Paulette Dufault, senior vice president for advertising and creative services, and Joseph Spellman, executive vice president for marketing development and creative services.   Kraft was most recently senior vice president and director of advertising and creative services at Calvin Klein Inc. in New York, where he worked on highly visible campaigns like the Calvin Klein underwear ads featuring the singer Marky Mark.   He also was vice president for advertising and marketing at Barneys New York and image director at Esprit de Corp. in San Francisco.   In an acquisition of one New York-based public relations agency by another, Ruder-Finn has bought the Kahn Communications Group. The terms were not disclosed. Kahn, which has billings estimated at $2 million from clients including AT&AMP;T and Liz Claiborne, will keep its name and move into Ruder-Finn's office in New York. Jeff Kahn, 36, chairman and creative director at Kahn, was named to the additional new position of president at Ruder-Finn Technology.   The following accounts have been awarded:   -- Ammirati &AMP; Puris/Lintas, New York, resigned the domestic portion of the Lexmark International account, with billings estimated at less than $5 million, as part of an effort to concentrate on larger accounts. The account was originally assigned to Lintas New York, a predecessor shop, in 1988. Ken Bissell, a spokesman for Lexmark in Lexington, Ky., said a review had begun.   -- Miles Inc., Elkhart, Ind., has assigned the accounts of two brands recently acquired from Sterling Health U.S.A., New York. McCann-Erickson New York was awarded Phillips' Milk of Magnesia, and Tatham Euro RSCG, Chicago, was awarded Midol pain reliever. Under Sterling, both accounts had been at Ammirati &AMP; Puris/Lintas, New York, until that agency resigned them because of a conflict.   -- Keycorp, Cleveland, to Borders, Perrin &AMP; Norrander, Seattle, to handle advertising for its Key Bank unit in states including Alaska and Washington, previously handled by J. Walter Thompson Chicago. "19888"	"I"	"believe"	" Moreover, whenever possible, such questions should be resolved before the court accepts the bond as security for collection of the judgment being appealed. After a debtor has benefitted from the postponement of collection of an adverse judgment, both that debtor and its successors-in-interest should normally be estopped from asserting that the judgment creditors who relied to their detriment on the validity of the bond had no right to do so. The very purpose of a supersedeas bond is to protect judgment creditors from the risk that insolvency of the debtor may impair their ability to enforce the judgment promptly. When the bond has served the purpose of forestalling immediate levies on the judgment debtor's assets levies that might have precipitated an earlier bankruptcy it is inequitable to postpone payment merely because the risk against which the bond was intended to provide protection has actually occurred. See id., at 319 (``It is manifestly unfair to force the judgment creditor to delay the right to collect with a promise to protect the judgment only to later refuse to allow that successful plaintiff to execute the bond because the debtor has sought protection under the laws of bankruptcy''); In re Southmark, 138 B. R. 820, 827-828 (Bkrtcy. Ct. ND Tex. 1992) (internal quotation marks omitted) (``The principal risk against which such bonds are intended as a protection is insolvency. To hold that the very contingency against which they guard shall, if it happens, discharge them, seems to us bad law and worse logic''). The inequity that the Court today condones does not, of course, demonstrate that its legal analysis is incorrect. It does, however, persuade me that the Court should not review this case as though it presented an ordinary collateral attack on an injunction entered by an Article III court. (footnote 22) Instead, the Court should, I believe, more carefully consider which of the two competing tribunals is guilty of trespassing in the other's domain. </P> "	"nyt_eng_199504.gz"

DALLAS-BIG HOME  DALLAS _ Even in ritzy north Dallas, where the limousines are stretched and the mansions massive, these dimensions are eye-popping. No oil baron, banker or Cowboy has built anything even remotely as large as the house that electronics magnate George Perrin and his wife will call home. Under construction barely a stone's throw from Ross Perot on Strait Lane, their 30,000-square-foot French chateau on six wooded acres will stand as the single largest residence in Big D. By ERNIE MAKOVY. Story has moved in ``a'' general news and ``l'' lifestyle categories.  BARRY CORBIN  FORT WORTH, Texas _ Actor Barry Corbin is rebounding nicely from the cancellation of ``Northern Exposure'' by appearing in films directed by Quentin Tarantino and Mario Van Peebles. He has also abandoned California and settled in on his spread outside Fort Worth. By MICHAEL H. PRICE. Story has moved in two takes in ``e'' entertainment category.  VIDEO-CAPSULE-REVIEWS  UNDATED _ Quick looks as films newly released on videotape, including ``Jefferson in Paris,'' ``Roommates,'' ``Losing Isaiah,'' ``Little Odessa,'' ``A Man of No Importance'' and ``The Madness of King George.'' By MICHAEL H. PRICE. Story has moved in ``e'' entertainment category.  MUSEUM-PHOTOS  FORT WORTH, Texas _ She never worked formally in the field. But, for nearly 25 years, anthropology has been the touchstone of Barbara McCandless' working life. McCandless was promoted recently to the new post of associate curator of photographs at the Amon Carter Museum, which means she oversees its nationally renowned collection of more than 350,000 photographic prints and negatives _ which are, for her, as much cultural documents as they are art objects. ``I've been in photography for 16 or 17 years,'' she says. ``But everything I do with photography, I end up thinking of as cultural anthropology.'' By JANET TYSON. Story has moved in ``e'' entertainment category.  CARS-SMITH-COLUMN  UNDATED _ There is a new Ford truck. But no, it is not THE new Ford truck. Most pickup fans are aware that there's a redesigned Ford in the not-too-distant future, but the all-new truck will be introduced after the first of the year as a 1997 model. For now, Ford is sticking with the current truck as its 1996-model pickup, with very few changes. To spot those changes, you'll need a scorecard. By STEVEN COLE SMITH. Story has moved in ``l'' lifestyle category.  COMPUTERS-BOMBAY-COLUMN  UNDATED _ Questions and usual sarcastic answers from our computer guy, this week about high-speed modems and old Works programs. By DR. EMILIO BOMBAY. Story has moved in ``l'' lifestyle category.  GARDENING-BROMELIADS  ARLINGTON, Texas _ There's only one thing wrong with bromeliads, from Flo Adams' point of view. They're addictive. Adams estimates that a couple of thousand of the exotic plants occupy her house, garden room and yard. Every fall, when they've spent all spring and summer growing and multiplying, she feels a bit overwhelmed. Freezing weather looms, and soon the plants will need to come inside. ``This time of year, I start sweating it,'' she said. ``I don't know if I can get 'em all back in.'' By CAROL NUCKOLS. Story has moved in ``l'' lifestyle category.  PETS-AMMONS-COLUMN  UNDATED _ Many people select dogs as pets because they're vocal. Barking, they believe, will deter strangers from their property. In time, it can also deter sleep or neighborly good will. Many cities and towns even have nuisance ordinances against barking. Dogs bark for several reasons, animal behavior experts say: to communicate with other dogs, threaten intruders, and/or say ``I'm bored,'' among several reasons. ``When we look at domestic dogs and an increase in barking, we've probably selected for it,'' says Dr. Bonnie Beaver, professor at Texas A&AMP;M University's College of Veterinary Medicine. By MARSHA Z. AMMONS. "44763"	"we"	"believe"	" This practice has some virtues. In an appropriate case, a GVR order conserves the scarce resources of this Court that might otherwise be expended on plenary consideration, assists the court below by flagging a particular issue that it does not appear to have fully considered, assists this Court by procuring the benefit of the lower court's insight before we rule on the merits, and alleviates the ``(p)otential for unequal treatment'' that is inherent in our inability to grant plenary review of all pending cases raising similar issues, see United States v. Johnson, 457 U. S. 537, 556, n. 16 (1982); cf. Griffith v. Kentucky, 479 U. S. 314, 323 (1987) (``we fulfill our judicial responsibility by instructing the lower courts to apply the new rule retroactively to cases not yet final''). Where intervening developments, or recent developments that we have reason to believe the court below did not fully consider, reveal a reasonable probability that the decision below rests upon a premise that the lower court would reject if given the opportunity for further consideration, and where it appears that such a redetermination may determine the ultimate outcome of the litigation, a GVR order is, we believe, potentially appropriate. Whether a GVR order is ultimately appropriate depends further on the equities of the case: if it appears that the intervening development, such as a confession of error in some but not all aspects of the decision below, is part of an unfair or manipulative litigation strategy, or if the delay and further cost entailed in a remand are not justified by the potential benefits of further consideration by the lower court, a GVR order is inappropriate. This approach is similar in its flexibility to this Court's longstanding approach to applications for stays and other summary remedies granted without determining the merits of the case under the All Writs Act, 28 U. S. C. s1651. See, e.g., Heckler v. Lopez, 463 U. S. 1328 (1983) (REHNQUIST, J., in chambers) (staying a District Court order pending the decision on the merits of the Court of Appeals). (Naturally, because GVR orders are premised on matters that we have reason to believe the court below did not fully consider, and because they require only further consideration, the standard that we apply in deciding whether to GVR is somewhat more liberal than the All Writs Act standard, under which relief is granted only upon a showing that a grant of certiorari and eventual reversal are probable, see id., at 1330.) Used in accordance with this approach, the GVR order can improve the fairness and accuracy of judicial outcomes while at the same time serving as a cautious and deferential alternative to summary reversal in cases whose precedential significance does not merit our plenary review. </P> "	"nyt_eng_199601.gz"

The order, Zenith's biggest for a product other than televisions, calls for the money-losing company to make at least 3 million digital set-top boxes for Americast, a joint venture between Walt Disney Co. and four regional Bell telephone companies. Production of the boxes, which are small computers attached to televisions that allow customers to receive programming, is slated to start in the first half of 1997. The contract is ``a launching point in the digital world'' for Zenith, said William Luehrs, president of Zenith's network systems division. Zenith, the nation's last TV maker, still depends on TV sales for about 90 percent of annual revenue. It sold a majority stake to a Korean electronics company last year and in April formed an alliance with U.S. Robotics Corp. to sell modems that provide cable-TV customers with fast access to computer on-line services and the global Internet network. Published 15:05 (See story: NI TLS) Sears Says It's Taking Action to Reduce Customers' Bad Debts By Lynne Marek  Washington: Sears Roebuck &AMP; Co. is taking aim at consumers who build up debts on its credit cards and then fail to pay. The department store operator said in a recent filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission that it implemented ``an aggressive action plan'' to cut back on its rising writeoffs of uncollectible debts. ``Components of the action plan include increases in collection staffing levels and additional investments in technology,'' the filing said. Sears' effort followed a 73 percent jump in uncollectible accounts in the second quarter, to $270 million from $156 million for the quarter last year. Rising cardholder bankruptcies and debt writeoffs accounted for the surge, the company said. The latest total includes $15 million the company put aside for future bad debt. Income from credit cards ``means a lot to the bottom line, and if you take this out it really hurts their profit,'' said Alan Creech, an analyst with Olde Discount Corp. Published 15:48 (See story: NI RET) Bundesbank Cuts Repo Rate, Cuts Across Europe Follow By Kathleen Sullivan  Frankfurt: The Bundesbank made an unexpectedly deep cut in a key German interest rate, setting off rate reductions across Europe aimed at boosting sluggish economies. Bundesbank President Hans Tietmeyer said slower money supply growth and an appropriate ``international environment'' allowed the central bank to cut its securities repurchase, or repo, rate by 30 basis points to a fixed 3.00 percent for the next two weeks. ``We will now observe further developments for some time,'' Tietmeyer said. ``We have, we believe, established clarity for the foreseeable future.'' The German cut -- coming amid deutsche mark gains against the U.S. dollar and European currencies -- was quickly followed by announcements of rate reductions in France, Belgium, the Netherlands and Austria. Bond and stock markets rose across Europe, while the deutsche mark slipped to a five-week low against the U.S. dollar. ``It's an expansive impulse for the European economies,'' said Gerhard Grebe, an economist at Bank Julius Baer (Deutschland) AG. Published 15:44 (See story: NI FRX) ALSO IN THE NEWS HSBC Americas to Buy First Federal Savings Rochester By Peter Moreira  Rochester, New York: HSBC Holdings Plc, the British parent company of Marine Midland Bank, said it will buy First Federal Savings and Loan Association for $620 million, continuing HSBC's expansion in the U.S. The acquisition of the Rochester, New York, thrift from CT Financial Services Inc. will provide $7.2 billion in assets and 79 branches in New York State to Marine Midland, which has about $22.7 billion in assets. It's a sign of continued U.S. growth for one of the few foreign banks that have succeeded in establishing a strong American retail banking presence. ``They have been expanding in this region, and I would expect them to continue to do so,'' said Katharine Rossow, a fixed-income banking analyst at Chase Securities. ``They're probably one of the most successful non-U.S. banks to operate a retail bank in this country.'' Martin  New York: Shanghai Petrochemical Co. chose Peregrine Brokerage Ltd. to arrange a HK$1.04 billion ($134 million) sale of stock, after Merrill Lynch &AMP; Co. was unable to find enough investors to complete a similar sale last month. China's biggest petrochemical company hired Hong Kong-based Peregrine, onia's leading investment banks, to sell 500 million shares at HK$2.075 each. The choice of Peregrine won't help Merrill's standing in Asia, where it is seeking to expand. The world's largest underwriter of stocks and bonds bought Smith New Court Plc for $842 million last year in part to boost its business in Asia. The sale comes as China's central bank today cut interest rates for the second time this year, giving a boost to Chinese stocks. ``Shanghai Petrochemical picked a good time for their sale,'' said Linda Johnston, who manages $300 million of Asian stocks at Guinness Flight Investment Management Ltd. in London. Published 15:57 (See story: NI HK)  Bad Timing for Pepsi's Latin Slip (Update1): Company Spotlight By Leslie Hillman  Caracas, Venezuela: The defection of the family that helped PepsiCo Inc. dominate Venezuela's soft-drink market for half a century to Coca-Cola Co. has its roots in a change that began 20 years ago. That's when PepsiCo started to diversify into snacks and restaurants and lost its fervor for soft drinks, says Gustavo Cisneros, a scion of the powerful Venezuelan family. ``Pepsi-Cola was a religion for them,'' he said. ``Then (the soda) was no longer that important.'' The family's stunning switch is just one of Pepsi's problems to crop up in the region. Last month, Pepsi took control of its Argentine bottler, the largest outside the U.S., and may need to spend $500 million to fix it. And two weeks ago, a franchisee in Puerto Rico turned in a loss following a skirmish with a Coke bottler and will restate its earnings. Published 16:40 (See story: NI BVG)  ECONOMY AND MARKETS Thursday's U.S. Markets: Stocks, Dollar Rise; Bonds Steady By Monique Wise  New York: U.S. stocks rallied as an unexpected cut in German interest rates promised faster economic growth in Europe and a jump in orders for U.S. exporters. ``We're living in a global economy, so if you get a revival somewhere else it helps us,'' said Mark Stumpp, chief investment officer at PDI Strategies in Short Hills, New Jersey, which manages assets of $22 billion. The Dow Jones Industrial Average climbed 43.65 to 5733.47. The Standard &AMP; Poor's 500 Index jumped 5.60 to 670.67, boosted most by oil, beverage and regional bank stocks, while the Nasdaq Composite Index soared 17.05 to 1143.91. U.S. bonds erased an early gain and were little changed as a rise in weekly claims for unemployment benefits did little to ease concern that a robust labor market will speed inflation later this year. The benchmark 30-year Treasury yield rose 1 basis point to 6.84 percent.  U.S. Economy: Jobless Claims Signal Employment Growth By Marthe Fourcade  Washington: An unusually low number of U.S. workers is applying for state unemployment benefits, suggesting the nation's job market remains vibrant. The report lends weight to analyst predictions the economy will add more than 200,000 new jobs this month. The government reported that weekly jobless claims rose 6,000 last week to 327,000, the third weekly increase in a row but less than the 350,000 that analysts say would require a lid on wage increases to ward off inflation. ``It's a very strong labor market,'' said Asha Bangalore, an economist at Northern Trust in Chicago. While other indicators show signs of an economic slowdown, the labor market ``is one the few sectors of the economy where you see unambiguous strength,'' Bangalore said. Since the beginning of the year, the U.S. economy has added more than 1 million jobs, with monthly job creation figures exceeding analysts' expectations throughout the spring. Times News Service  NEW YORK _ Lest there be any doubt that the fall book party season is moving into full swing, consider two gatherings taking place Monday evening.  A crowd of about 250, ranging from Toni Morrison, the Nobel Prize-winning novelist, to Ralph Branca, the former Brooklyn Dodger pitcher, is expected at the Celeste Bartos Forum of the New York Public Library to honor Rachel Robinson. Her book about her husband, ``Jackie Robinson: An Intimate Portrait,'' is being published by Harry N. Abrams.  Next year will mark the 50th since Robinson joined the Brooklyn Dodgers and broke the barriers keeping black athletes out of the major leagues. Writers and documentary filmmakers have deluged his widow with proposals for projects commemorating the anniversary.  ``I knew that they didn't know the man I knew, and I decided I wanted to contribute my picture of him,'' she said the other day.  That picture includes 300 photographs winnowed from a collection of 700 and ranging from one taken on their first date in 1941 to those from his funeral in 1972.  Mrs. Robinson had always been the keeper of the family photo albums and scrapbooks, and she said, ``I knew that photos were so evocative that they would lead me into the story.'' The accompanying text traces his life in detail and with candor about both triumph and tragedy.  ``Some people, especially youngsters, think that Jack disappeared after playing ball,'' she said.  ``They don't know how involved he was in the civil rights movement nor that he was a political activist. I want people to decide for themselves what he was, but I also want them to know that he was a man committed to his race, to a more equitable society, and to his family. They know the aggressive player, they don't know the tender and loving husband and father.''  c.1996 N.Y. Times News Service  NEW YORK _ Marlo Thomas and Paul Newman are rounding up their friends to pay tribute Monday night to Dr. Helen Caldicott, the Australian pediatrician who has waged long battles against nuclear weapons and the degradation of the environment.  Dr. Caldicott has written an autobiography, ``A Desperate Passion,'' which is being published by W.W. Norton. The book is a look back at her causes _ she founded Physicians for Social Responsibility _ and the heavy personal toll she paid for her crusades. Her husband of 27 years left her and she suffered bouts of depression and spiritual crises.  ``She was an ordinary woman with an ordinary life who became an extraordinary fighter for nuclear disarmament,'' Ms. Thomas said the other day. ``She gave up her life to travel the world.''  Guests who have said they will be at the party in Ms. Thomas' Manhattan apartment range from Bianca Jagger to Sister Helen Prejean, the Roman Catholic nun whose struggle against capital punishment became the theme of the film ``Dead Man Walking.'' </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960915.0105 type=story > <HEADLINE> SOME SEE REEVES AS COWBOYS' COACH NEXT SEASON </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> EAST RUTHERFORD, N.J. Co-author Michael Klepper told the New York Times that he and Robert Gunther compiled their list by comparing the wealth of individuals with the gross national product of the times in which they lived. ``Most of the great fortunes were made during the post-Civil War era through Reconstruction,'' said Klepper, ``before income taxes and antitrust legislation.''  John D. Rockefeller is first on the list.  Washington's fortune was valued at $530,000, 1/777th of the economy, derived from real estate he acquired when he married the wealthy widow Martha Custis. Bill Gates, who has between $15 billion and $20 billion, represents 1/425th of the economy.  As to other presidential possibilities, ``Ross Perot was not even close to being on our list,'' Klepper said. ``In fact, he came closer to becoming president of the United States.''  IN PURSUIT OF JUSTICE FOR ALL  The Oracle, weekly newsletter of the mega-law firm Orrick, Herrington &AMP; Sutcliffe, congratulates the crew: ``1996 was a record year for Orrick in many ways and not without a record level of effort on the part of attorneys, legal assistants and staff firmwide _ particularly in the very busy month of December.''  And how were the interests of justice so ably served? The newsletter zeroes in on a singular element: ``In one area of critical importance, collection efficiency, the partner-in-charge had high praise for efforts throughout the firm.''  A BROTHER'S FOND RECOLLECTION  Paul Theroux says that his book, ``My Other Life: A Novel'' (Houghton Mifflin; $24.95), is ``the story of a life I could have lived, an imaginary memoir.'' The star of the book is a character named Paul Theroux, and many of its anecdotes involve real people. The question, says the latest Civilization magazine, is whether the author has the right to include real people in what may be a work of fiction.  After a segment appeared in the New Yorker, the writer's ex-wife, Anne Theroux, complained in a letter to the editor about ``a very unpleasant character with my name'' who ``said and did things that I have never said or done.''  The author's brother, Alexander Theroux, was even stronger. ``We in the family don't mind his affected gentility,'' he wrote in Boston magazine, ``his smug and self-important airs, his urgent starf_ insistence that he's a friend of lords and ladies, and only laugh at the fame he courts, the self-aggrandizement, inviting celebrities like Jane Pauley and Bryant Gumbel to his house, neither of whom, I believe, has ever quite managed to make it.'' "133109"	"I"	"believe"	" $$$ </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021207.0073 type=multi > <HEADLINE> NEWS OF THE WEEK IN REVIEW </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 12-07-02 13:37:00EST (BC-THISWEEK-REVIEW-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> SURPRISE, IT'S THE ECONOMY AGAIN, STUPID!  If you measure the success of a president by job creation, George W. Bush is a failure so far. The economy now supports 2.2 million fewer private-sector jobs than it did when he was sworn into office.  That fact didn't bother voters in last month's elections, but the president decided that he needs a new economic policy team. On Friday, as some disappointing employment numbers were released, both Treasury Secretary Paul H. O'Neill and Lawrence B. Lindsey, the president's chief economic adviser, were pushed out.  To whom Bush will turn is unclear. O'Neill had been criticized for off-the-cuff comments that sometimes unsettled currency markets, and for not having the feel for financial markets of Robert E. Rubin, President Bill Clinton's Treasury secretary.  O'Neill had been among the Bush administration officials who seemed most concerned about budget deficits, and most sanguine about the immediate economic outlook. If the Bush administration wants to sell a new economic stimulus package _ and its budget deficits _ it probably needed officials who could advocate the policy with more conviction.  That stimulus may be a good idea was indicated by Friday's job reports. It was no surprise that the number of manufacturing jobs in the United States shrank. That figure has been falling for years. But the weakness in service industries is new. The number of private-sector service jobs edged up by 7,000 in November, but is still 72,000 lower than it was two years ago, when Bush was elected.  Not since 1958 has the service sector lost jobs over a two-year period, not even during the severe recessions of 1973-75 and 1981-82. It is easy to understand why the administration might feel this was not the time to wait and hope for a better economy.  OF MICE AND MEN  Man may not consider himself much like the mouse, but at the level of their genomes the two species look very much the same, according to a report in Nature last week. Each has 30,000 or so genes, inherited from a common ancestor who lived 75 million years ago, just as mammals were entering the stage and dinosaurs were about to exit.  But looked at closely, the mouse's genome has many specialties that the human genome does not. The consortium of scientists that examined the mouse genome found 25 cases in which humans have a single gene and the mouse a cluster of related genes.  The clusters arise because in the course of evolution one gene has been accidentally copied several times over, and the copies, after mutation, have all found useful roles to play. In the mouse, 14 of these gene clusters are related to reproduction, liking making pheromones, the aphrodisiac scents that control the mouse's mating behavior, and forming the copulatory plug, a device used by male mice to prevent their inamorata being made to conceive by rivals. The emphasis on reproduction is not surprising given how rodents and primates differ in their mating habits.  Once evolution has devised a good thing, it sticks with it. All forms of life, from bacteria to humans, have the same basic set of cellular housekeeping genes. All multicellular animals share another batch of genes, largely for cells to communicate with each other. Then there's another set of genes developed for life as a mammal. Less than one percent of the mouse's genes are unique to rodents.  To make a mouse, in other words, scientists can now see that evolution took the generic recipe of mammal-making genes, sprinkled in just a dash of special-purpose mouse genes and eek! _ another wonderfully designed organism from nature's magic cookbook.  NEWS IN BRIEF  SHOW THEM THE WEAPONS: At the White House, the mood came down to this: When it comes to Iraq, there are lies, damn lies ... and inspections. Ari Fleischer, the White House spokesman, responded to Iraq's claims that it possesses no weapons of mass destruction by declaring that ``Iraq has lied before, and they're lying now.'' He and Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld said that inspections, which began last week, were bound to fail unless Saddam Hussein led the United Nations to the country's hidden weapons caches and to the scientists and engineers who developed them. The White House is betting Saddam will not meet that high standard, but it is unclear whether the rest of the uneasy coalition against Iraq will agree with President Bush's standard of what constitutes a ``material breach'' _ and a cause for war.  SURPRISING ARRESTS: Indonesia, Southeast Asia's laggard in the campaign against terrorism, is rounding up suspects in the Bali bombing at a rate that surprises, and pleases, foreign governments. The police seized Ali Ghufron, better known as Mukhlas, who is thought to be an operational commander for Jemaah Islamiyah, a regional Islamic network. Indonesian officials are confident that Mukhlas will lead them to Hambali, a reputed senior al-Qaida operative, whose fingerprints are on attacks against the United States going back a decade.  PERSUADING TURKEY: If President Bush decides to use force against Saddam Hussein, the Pentagon wants to use Turkey's bases to mount bombing raids and deploy ground troops. But as Deputy Defense Secretary Paul D. Wolfowitz discovered during a trip to Ankara, Turkish officials are reluctant to allow large numbers of troops, and it wants the U.N. Security Council to pass a new resolution authorizing force, which could delay military action. U.S. officials hope to appeal at the highest level. The leader of Turkey's governing party, Recep Tayyip Erdogan, has been invited to Washington to meet President Bush this week.  MIXED SIGNALS IN MIDEAST: After 26 months of Palestinian suicide bombings and pitiless Israeli retaliation, is there light at the end of the tunnel? Prime Minister Ariel Sharon of Israel said that the so-called road map to Palestinian statehood was ``reasonable, realistic and, I believe, attainable,'' with some changes. Sen. Joseph R. Biden Jr., D-Del., the chairman of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee, talked to Palestinian leaders in Tel Aviv and said that he saw a new yearning for peace among Arabs as well. But officially, Palestinians rejected Sharon's statement as nothing new. And a Palestinian branch of al-Qaida posted a message on a Web site announcing its formation. Its involvement could plunge the search for peace back into darkness.  PADILLA GETS A LAWYER: A federal judge in New York ruled that Jose Padilla, who has been held incommunicado as an enemy combatant in a Navy brig since June, may consult with lawyers challenging his detention. Padilla is suspected of plotting to explode a radioactive bomb in the United States. But the judge affirmed President Bush's power to detain enemy combatants, including Americans captured on U.S. soil, and said the government needed to make only a minimal showing of proof to justify such action.  NO HELP FOR UNITED: The Bush administration refused to give United Airlines, the nation's second-largest air carrier, a $1.8 billion loan guarantee, almost certainly pushing it into bankruptcy court. A federal panel created last year to help airlines deal with the aftershocks of the Sept. 11 attacks declared that the company's business plan was ``financially unsound.'' American Airlines and Continental lobbied hard against the loan guarantee, but the airline is expected to keep flying for at least the near term, even if it seeks bankruptcy protection. And the panel said it can still submit a revised proposal.  HIGH COURT REVISITS: Babies born in 1978, the year the Supreme Court last considered and narrowly upheld affirmative action in university admissions, have grown up and graduated from college, and still the issue remains unresolved in society and the law. Now the court will hear challenges to two affirmative action programs at the University of Michigan, deciding whether racial diversity is a ``compelling'' government interest and if so, whether these programs are drawn as narrowly as possible to accomplish that goal.  The court also agreed to revisit the issue of gay rights, accepting a gay couple's challenge to a Texas criminal sodomy law.  THE CARDINAL'S PAPERS:  Newly released documents show that the Archdiocese of Boston allowed priests accused of sexual abuse to continue working in ministry, apparently dismissing or minimizing allegations against them. The documents show Cardinal Bernard H. Law made decisions about accused priests and wrote several priests sympathetic letters, as late as a few years ago. The archdiocese's finance council also gave the church the go-ahead to file for bankruptcy as a way to deal with the hundreds of lawsuits it faces. The archdiocese has not yet decided if it will seek bankruptcy protection, something no other diocese has ever done. The decision would be subject to Vatican approval.  AOL REVAMPED: AOL Time Warner revealed its latest plan to revive its AOL division, in part by selling a version of its service to high-speed Internet users. The company said it would deliver exclusive video and sound, partly through the company's other divisions, like Warner Brothers' film and music studios.  CLINTON SPEAKS, KERRY EXPLORES: Was there a Democrat left who had not lamented the results of the midterm elections? As it turned out, yes, there was _ former President Bill Clinton, who in a New York speech told his party that Democrats had ceded to Republicans the one issue that has captivated the American public: national security. As it turned out, his counsel came the same week that Sen. John F. Kerry, D-Mass., set up a presidential exploratory committee. Kerry, a Vietnam veteran and a member of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee, made clear that national security and foreign affairs would be central issues he would take to President Bush should he win his party's nomination.  $12 MILLION GIFT, WITH STRINGS: Peter B. Lewis, the outspoken philanthropist from Cleveland, resorted to ``tough love'' at the Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum. As chairman, he forced Thomas Krens, the museum's director, to cut the operating budget to one-half of its level just a few years ago. Then, Lewis gave the museum $12 million _ enough to pay outstanding bills and start the new year on a frugal note.  CENTRAL PARK RAPE: An old criminal case came out of storage when the Manhattan district attorney asked a judge to drop the convictions of five men who confessed in 1989 to being involved with the rape of a jogger in Central Park, along with a series of other attacks. A convicted murderer and serial rapist, Matias Reyes, stepped forward in January to claim that he alone assaulted the woman. Prosecutors found his story, backed by DNA tests, persuasive. In Philadelphia on Thursday, he will talk about his domestic agenda. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021207.0074 type=multi > <HEADLINE> NEWS OF THE WEEK IN REVIEW </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 12-07-02 13:37:00EST (BC-THISWEEK-REVIEW-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> SURPRISE, IT'S THE ECONOMY AGAIN, STUPID!  If you measure the success of a president by job creation, George W. Bush is a failure so far. The economy now supports 2.2 million fewer private-sector jobs than it did when he was sworn into office.  That fact didn't bother voters in last month's elections, but the president decided that he needs a new economic policy team. On Friday, as some disappointing employment numbers were released, both Treasury Secretary Paul H. O'Neill and Lawrence B. Lindsey, the president's chief economic adviser, were pushed out.  To whom Bush will turn is unclear. O'Neill had been criticized for off-the-cuff comments that sometimes unsettled currency markets, and for not having the feel for financial markets of Robert E. Rubin, President Bill Clinton's Treasury secretary.  O'Neill had been among the Bush administration officials who seemed most concerned about budget deficits, and most sanguine about the immediate economic outlook. If the Bush administration wants to sell a new economic stimulus package _ and its budget deficits _ it probably needed officials who could advocate the policy with more conviction.  That stimulus may be a good idea was indicated by Friday's job reports. It was no surprise that the number of manufacturing jobs in the United States shrank. That figure has been falling for years. But the weakness in service industries is new. The number of private-sector service jobs edged up by 7,000 in November, but is still 72,000 lower than it was two years ago, when Bush was elected.  Not since 1958 has the service sector lost jobs over a two-year period, not even during the severe recessions of 1973-75 and 1981-82. It is easy to understand why the administration might feel this was not the time to wait and hope for a better economy.  OF MICE AND MEN  Man may not consider himself much like the mouse, but at the level of their genomes the two species look very much the same, according to a report in Nature last week. Each has 30,000 or so genes, inherited from a common ancestor who lived 75 million years ago, just as mammals were entering the stage and dinosaurs were about to exit.  But looked at closely, the mouse's genome has many specialties that the human genome does not. The consortium of scientists that examined the mouse genome found 25 cases in which humans have a single gene and the mouse a cluster of related genes.  The clusters arise because in the course of evolution one gene has been accidentally copied several times over, and the copies, after mutation, have all found useful roles to play. In the mouse, 14 of these gene clusters are related to reproduction, liking making pheromones, the aphrodisiac scents that control the mouse's mating behavior, and forming the copulatory plug, a device used by male mice to prevent their inamorata being made to conceive by rivals. The emphasis on reproduction is not surprising given how rodents and primates differ in their mating habits.  Once evolution has devised a good thing, it sticks with it. All forms of life, from bacteria to humans, have the same basic set of cellular housekeeping genes. All multicellular animals share another batch of genes, largely for cells to communicate with each other. Then there's another set of genes developed for life as a mammal. Less than one percent of the mouse's genes are unique to rodents.  To make a mouse, in other words, scientists can now see that evolution took the generic recipe of mammal-making genes, sprinkled in just a dash of special-purpose mouse genes and eek! _ another wonderfully designed organism from nature's magic cookbook.  NEWS IN BRIEF  SHOW THEM THE WEAPONS: At the White House, the mood came down to this: When it comes to Iraq, there are lies, damn lies ... and inspections. Ari Fleischer, the White House spokesman, responded to Iraq's claims that it possesses no weapons of mass destruction by declaring that ``Iraq has lied before, and they're lying now.'' He and Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld said that inspections, which began last week, were bound to fail unless Saddam Hussein led the United Nations to the country's hidden weapons caches and to the scientists and engineers who developed them. The White House is betting Saddam will not meet that high standard, but it is unclear whether the rest of the uneasy coalition against Iraq will agree with President Bush's standard of what constitutes a ``material breach'' _ and a cause for war.  SURPRISING ARRESTS: Indonesia, Southeast Asia's laggard in the campaign against terrorism, is rounding up suspects in the Bali bombing at a rate that surprises, and pleases, foreign governments. The police seized Ali Ghufron, better known as Mukhlas, who is thought to be an operational commander for Jemaah Islamiyah, a regional Islamic network. Indonesian officials are confident that Mukhlas will lead them to Hambali, a reputed senior al-Qaida operative, whose fingerprints are on attacks against the United States going back a decade.  PERSUADING TURKEY: If President Bush decides to use force against Saddam Hussein, the Pentagon wants to use Turkey's bases to mount bombing raids and deploy ground troops. But as Deputy Defense Secretary Paul D. Wolfowitz discovered during a trip to Ankara, Turkish officials are reluctant to allow large numbers of troops, and it wants the U.N. Security Council to pass a new resolution authorizing force, which could delay military action. U.S. officials hope to appeal at the highest level. The leader of Turkey's governing party, Recep Tayyip Erdogan, has been invited to Washington to meet President Bush this week.  MIXED SIGNALS IN MIDEAST: After 26 months of Palestinian suicide bombings and pitiless Israeli retaliation, is there light at the end of the tunnel? Prime Minister Ariel Sharon of Israel said that the so-called road map to Palestinian statehood was ``reasonable, realistic and, I believe, attainable,'' with some changes. Sen. Joseph R. Biden Jr., D-Del., the chairman of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee, talked to Palestinian leaders in Tel Aviv and said that he saw a new yearning for peace among Arabs as well. But officially, Palestinians rejected Sharon's statement as nothing new. And a Palestinian branch of al-Qaida posted a message on a Web site announcing its formation. Its involvement could plunge the search for peace back into darkness.  PADILLA GETS A LAWYER: A federal judge in New York ruled that Jose Padilla, who has been held incommunicado as an enemy combatant in a Navy brig since June, may consult with lawyers challenging his detention. Padilla is suspected of plotting to explode a radioactive bomb in the United States. But the judge affirmed President Bush's power to detain enemy combatants, including Americans captured on U.S. soil, and said the government needed to make only a minimal showing of proof to justify such action.  NO HELP FOR UNITED: The Bush administration refused to give United Airlines, the nation's second-largest air carrier, a $1.8 billion loan guarantee, almost certainly pushing it into bankruptcy court. A federal panel created last year to help airlines deal with the aftershocks of the Sept. 11 attacks declared that the company's business plan was ``financially unsound.'' American Airlines and Continental lobbied hard against the loan guarantee, but the airline is expected to keep flying for at least the near term, even if it seeks bankruptcy protection. And the panel said it can still submit a revised proposal.  HIGH COURT REVISITS: Babies born in 1978, the year the Supreme Court last considered and narrowly upheld affirmative action in university admissions, have grown up and graduated from college, and still the issue remains unresolved in society and the law. Now the court will hear challenges to two affirmative action programs at the University of Michigan, deciding whether racial diversity is a ``compelling'' government interest and if so, whether these programs are drawn as narrowly as possible to accomplish that goal.  The court also agreed to revisit the issue of gay rights, accepting a gay couple's challenge to a Texas criminal sodomy law.  THE CARDINAL'S PAPERS:  Newly released documents show that the Archdiocese of Boston allowed priests accused of sexual abuse to continue working in ministry, apparently dismissing or minimizing allegations against them. The documents show Cardinal Bernard H. Law made decisions about accused priests and wrote several priests sympathetic letters, as late as a few years ago. The archdiocese's finance council also gave the church the go-ahead to file for bankruptcy as a way to deal with the hundreds of lawsuits it faces. The archdiocese has not yet decided if it will seek bankruptcy protection, something no other diocese has ever done. The decision would be subject to Vatican approval.  AOL REVAMPED: AOL Time Warner revealed its latest plan to revive its AOL division, in part by selling a version of its service to high-speed Internet users. The company said it would deliver exclusive video and sound, partly through the company's other divisions, like Warner Brothers' film and music studios. My wife, Jeri, lunges forward to change the channel, but I wave her off.  ``I like that song,'' I protest as she shoots me a look that (to steal a line from P.G. Wodehouse) would open an oyster at 50 paces.  Yes, yes, I know. ``Sugar Sugar%'(cy the Archies stands alone and unchallenged atop the all-time list of ghastly pop songs. It's one of the tunes the U.S. Army's Psych Ops forces once blared at former Panamanian leadersakes. The Archies were a quintet of cartoon characters with, if I remember correctly, Jughead on drums.  Still _ when ``Sugar Sugar'' pops up on the radio, I don't think of any of that. I'm transported back to the summer of 1969, when my mom, my sisters, my friend Greg and I were packed into an overloaded Ford Fairlane station wagon en route to D.L. Bliss State Park at Lake Tahoe. Cringe if you will at the brain-damaging lyrics (``Sugar sugar, ah honey honey/ You are my candy girl/ And you've got me wanting you`') but to me they bring to mind pine-scented mountain air, the mustiness of an old canvas tent and a Pavlovian salivation at the thought of s'mores.  Never underestimate the power of a bad pop song. As Dave Barry once wrote, ``Your brain, as part of its lifelong effort to drive you insane, insists on remembering the songs you hate and playing them over and over and over.'' But, if you let them, these tunes can summon up surprisingly vivid travel memories, in much the same way certain smells evoke a much deeper, more sensual recollection than a photo ever could. Like it or not, these inescapably grating tunes are the subconscious sound tracks of our lives.  As anyone who's traveled outside the United States knows, bad American pop songs are almost as ubiquitous an export as bad American movies. The more saccharine the tune, the more imbecilic the lyrics, the faster it races up the pop charts from Kiev to Kuala Lumpur. Appallingly awful pop songs, apparently, are the world's language.  You can let these songs drive you mad, or you can do what I do: treat them as souvenirs of your travels _ cthat Lake Tahoe camping trip are ``In the Year 2525'' by Zager and Evans (``Ain't gonna need your teeth, won't need your eyes/ You won't find a thing to chew/ Nobody's gonna look at you'') and ``Young Girl'' by Gary Puckett and The Union Gap (``[o}733irl, get out of my mind/ My love for you is way out of line.'') But that one aCvu66 10627::0 20920964299760 12$617nfusing it with the 1969 Gary Puckett hit, ``This Girl is a Woman Now,'' which, as Barry points out, is almost exactly the same song.  ``Good Morning Starshine'' by Oliver has lyrics that make ``Sugar Sugar'' sound like Cole Porter (``Gliddy glib gloopy/Nibby nabby noopy''), but to me it's the souvenir of another family camping trip, to Yosemite. When I hear it, I see cloud shadows scudding across the face of Half Dome and feel the cool tingle in the air after a crackling, booming thunderstorm. Let others jam their fingers in their ears and make yucky faces; the song always makes me smile.  New Zealanders are among my favorite people on Earth, not least because of their wholehearted, unapologetic embrace of syrupy pop songs. It goes with their unaffected, un-ironic, un-postmodernist national character. To the rest of us these tunes are guilty pleasures; to the Kiwis they're merely pleasures. New Zealand was the first country I ever visited outside the United States, the place where the wanderlust bug bit me good and hard. On that trip, in 1982, the song everyone was humming was the almost unbearably chirpy ``Hands Up!'' (``Hands up, baby, hands up/ Gimme your heart, gimme gimme your heart, gimme gimme''), which, I believe, at the time was the theme song for the All Blacks, the national rugby team. Whenever I hear it, I think of white-eyebrowed men in tweed jackets with matching shorts and knee socks, Maori bus drivers with Cockney accents and that feeling of the whole world opening before me, like a fabulous present I was unwrapping. (To others, ``Hands Up!'' no doubt conjures memories of Club Med; it's become their unofficial theme song.) Several years later, when I returned to New Zealand, the No.1 song on the pop charts was Madonna's ``True Blue'' (``I've had other lips/ I've sailed a thousand ships/ But no matter where I go/ You're the one for me, baby, this I know''), a sugary ditty that even hard-core Madonna fans in this country disown. No matter. When I hear it I'm mentally downing the last sip of DB Lager and getting ready to go tramping on the Routeburn Track.  Janet Jackson's ``Nasty Boys'' (``I don't like no nasty car/ I don't like a nasty food, huh/ Ooh ooh yeah/ The only nasty thing I like is the nasty groove, huh'') sends me back to 1986 and a wretched London hostel where Jeri and I began our first around-the-world adventure. There were speakers in the hallways, and they always seemed to be blaring that song. When I hear it now, I'm wrapped again in my cheap towel, padding down the hallway to the moldy communal shower. (And such is the cleansing power of memory that even this thought brings a grin to my face.)  In the summer of 1990, Chris DeBurgh's ``Lady in Red'' (``I've never seen you looking so lovely as you did tonight/ I've never seen you shine so bright'') seemed to be on every radio in the Mediterranean world, and when I hear it now I'm mentally strolling through the faded lobby of the Grand Hotel in Cairo, hopping off a Greek ferry on the island of Paros or riding a long-distance Turkish bus, slathering on the sweet-smelling lemon cologne they pass around.  Far from covering my ears when these songs come on the radio, I've actually sought them out. I've burned them all onto a handy CD I can popffer it to the Pentagon in case they ever need to smoke Osama bin Laden out of his cave. He addressed the annual convention of the American Society of Newspaper Editors, which includes executives of more than 1,500 papers.  Hours earlier, 68 people were killed when a series of car bombs detonated in the British-controlled city of Basra in southern Iraq and at least four people were killed in a suicide bombing at Saudi national police headquarters in Riyadh.  ``There was a terrorist act. ... And they just blew up innocent Iraqis. Well, they attacked in Riyadh, Saudi Arabia. ... And they attack all the time,'' Bush said. ``They'd like to attack us again as well, by the way.''  Although his prevailing message was serious, Bush at times was jocular. It was one of the rare instances as president where Bush addressed a large group not dominated by his own supporters.  Bush, who has said he prefers to get his news from trusted aides, playfully addressed the newspaper executives as ``members of the Politburo,'' the central committee that controlled the Communist Party in the former Soviet Union. And he joked that he welcomed a chance to duck their questions.  Bush was asked how he will convince Americans the war against terrorism can be won.  ``I can understand why they think we're going to get hit again. They saw what happened in Madrid. This is a hard country to defend,'' Bush said.  After outlining efforts by the Homeland Security Department and others to safeguard the nation and extolling the Patriot Act, Bush concluded, ``We just got to stay on the offense, is what we've got to do.''  He urged the editors to see the movie ``Osama,'' the story of a young girl in Afghanistan during the Taliban rule who disguises herself as a boy to earn money for her family.  ``It's hard for the American mentality to grasp how barbaric the Taliban was toward women in Afghanistan. , they said,	 NASA shocked the world by announcing in 1996 that ALH8004 holds several clues showing Martian life once existed. These include carbon compounds, magnetic minerals and more complex hydrocarbons similar to those deposited by Earth bacteria. Astonishingly, it also contains minute structures that look as if they could be fossil bacteria. The chemicals were deposited in the rock about 3.5million years ago, about the same time primitive life was developing on Earth. The NASA team said that, taken together, tiew33skeptical of the claims made for ALH8004. The chemicals, they said, could have come from non-living processes. The ``fossils'' are much smaller than most bacteria on Earth and could be simply mineral deposits. (But, as paleontologist Henry Gee has pointed out, we have no good reason to judge what alien fossils might look like based solely on earthly examples.) </P> 	nyt_eng_200404.gz

MORE FOREIGN VISITORS TO U.S.  Leisure and business travelers to the United States from overseas rose 5 percent in January over January 2003, according to the Commerce Department. The biggest national increase, 11 percent, came from Britons. Japanese arrivals rose 9 percent in January and visits from Germany were up 8 percent. Meanwhile, analysts at the World Travel and Tourism meeting in Qatar predicted that as foreign travel expands into new markets, the United States will continue to see growth in overseas visitors in the coming decade, while the growth rate of Americans going abroad should remain relatively flat.  PHILADELPHIA HERE THEY COME  Reacting, as expected, to the arrival of Southwest Airlines, which starts operations in Philadelphia next week, US Airways slashed fares at its hub there and issued a statement noting that US Air, unlike Southwest, is a network carrier with routes offering ''nonstop service to 106 destinations`` in North America and ''10 European destinations from Philadelphia.`` In announcing lower fares (and the abandonment of Saturday night stay requirements) to battle Southwest on competitive routes, US Air also noted that its customers get ''advance seat assignments.`` Colleen Barrett, the president and chief operating officer of Southwest, which is known for its ''cattle call`` seating in which passengers take any available seat, described her company as ''simply ecstatic`` about coming to Philadelphia.  SONG ADDS FRILLS  Increasingly, low-fare airlines are adding amenities even as their network competitors cut back. The committee's cHaibman, GaryA.0Hack, dean of the School of Design at the University of Pennsylvania, wrote a four-page letter of recommendations March 10, but nearly two months later few people have seen it.  This includes Rob Tuchman, the Hale and Dorr lawyer who has co-chaired Mayor Menino's Artery Completion Task Force for years. ``This is a stealth organization,'' Tuchman said. The committee and the Turnpike Authority seem so hobbled by process considerations, he added, that ``we are losing all the benefit of the substance.''  And benefit there is, at least potentially.  The Hack letter, after praising much of the design work that has gone into the two North End xaRo parcels straddling Hanover Street, {ays he new parks is ``not well integrated with the park design,'' he writes.  The YMCA building needs public access and room for pedestrian passage on both sides of the parcel, the letter says. And the Bulfinch Triangle area just south of North Station should be developed with buildings having multiple entrances to encourage street life.  Overall, the letter emphasizes the committee's conviction about ``how vitally important it is to maintain a sense of continuity along the entire length of the corridor and connecting to the Charles River Basin.''  These and other recommendations from the Design Advisory Committee are significant and deserve a wider airing. With design work proceeding on various parts of the greenway, every passing week lessens the opportunity for the level of inspired coordination this project must have if it is to reach its potential.  For instance, the current design work is going forward under contracts that focus on the new park parcels themselves so the design teams are not putting as much effort as they should into coordinating with adjacent properties. Likewise, there needs to be better-integrated planning that pulls the four sets of park parcels, the ramp parcels, the development parcels, and the adjacent properties into a whole that is designed and programmed with high ambition.  Hack's letter calls for the greenway to be ``of an international design standard in terms of quality, identity, implementation, and durability.''  It still can be that, but not if the goal is to get the job finished with minimal fuss and at minimal cost. That happy philosophy </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20040701.0136 type=advis > <HEADLINE> BEACH POST GRAPHICS DATABASE HOW ROD GOES MOD ON A NEW WAVE OF FAME </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> WEST PALM BEACH, Fla. </DATELINE> <TEXT> WEST PALM BEACH, Fla. -- He's a legendary rocker who's riding a new wave of fame thanks to his albums of American standards. And he's a jet-setting, spike-haired playboy who's been involved with such high-profile women as Swedish film star Britt Ekland and model Rachel Hunter.  These days, Stewart, who regularly vacations in Palm Beach, probably needs such opportunities for escape. In the past few years, his career has taken an unexpectedly busy turn, thanks to his platinum-selling albums of standards, It Had to Be You: The Great American Songbook and As Time Goes By: The Great American Songbook Volume II.  A third volume, featuring such songs as Stardust, Blue Moon and 'S Wonderful, is in the works.  I do believe it's better than the other two put together, Stewart says without hesitation. The first one I did -- I sang with no confidence. As the albums have come to be blockbusters, I've gone, 'Wow, people actually like it.' So I'm much more comfortable with the material.  Which is not to say that Stewart, whose classic hits include Maggie May, You Wear It Well, Downtown Train and You're in My Heart, has forsaken his rock 'n' roll past.  Among his current projects is an album with Rolling Stones guitarist Ron Wood, whose friendship with Stewart goes back to their days in the '70s British band Faces.  It's called 'You Strum and I'll Sing,'  Stewart says of the long-simmering recording, though he concedes we'll be in wheelchairs by the time it comes out.  A Faces boxed set  Also in the works: a four-CD Faces retrospective, which Stewart calls amazing.  I was listening to it for two hours yesterday. They've dug up some wonderful BBC archive tapes. (Faces keyboardist) Ian McLagan spent four years putting this stuff together, Stewart reports. It might sound biased, but so be it: It's the best boxed set I've ever seen.  Perhaps it's not an empty boast, given the growing acclaim for Faces, a band that also included drummer Kenny Jones, who later joined The Who. But why did the group, whose hits included Stewart's classic Stay With Me, have such a short stint of six years?  I think we took it as far as we could, mainly because if we stayed together, we'd all be dead. Our livers would have packed up, Stewart recalls of the band's reckless run.  Still, Stewart's distinct interpretive abilities were largely shaped during his time with Faces. He says there's no particular trick to finding the right song for his raggedly telling voice.  It's almost like trying on a suit and a pair of shoes and a shirt and tie. All the components (have to fit): the lyrics, the melody, what the song is about, he says.  The challenge becomes all the greater when it comes to the great American songbook, Stewart adds. You can get some of these songs from that era that are a little bit too -- how can I say? -- a little too Liberace, a little too Broadwayish. Most of the songs I've done on all three albums have got a little edge to them.  Stewart includes a selection of standards on his current tour, replete with a set -- in the second half of the show -- that recalls the Big Band ballroom era. But he doesn't neglect his other standards -- that is, the rock and pop ones.  And he brings back an old favorite from his catalog -- Cat Stevens' The First Cut Is the Deepest, which was recently made popular again by Sheryl Crow.  She did a good version, Stewart says. But I thought if Sheryl has done it and it's become a Top 10 hit, I just want people to know that I did it first.  A deeper appreciation  Actually, it's something of a small miracle that Stewart is singing at all these days. In 2000, he underwent surgery for thyroid cancer -- and his voice was nearly lost forever.  It really was scary, he recalls. It came just out of the blue. I was lucky, because they caught it early, and I was in and out of the hospital in 24 hours. (But) there was a point I couldn't sing a bloody note. You almost have to re-teach yourself how to sing. When they cut through the muscles in your throat, you go through what they call muscle memory loss. Your muscles forget how to tell your vocal box what to do.  But when he recovered, Stewart found a hidden benefit: His voice had deepened. It's given me a (certain) tonality, which is why I can do these American songbook things. I don't think I could have done them 20 years ago, he says.  Stewart's new life also includes a relatively new beau. He's been with Penny Lancaster, a leggy model and photographer, for about five years. (They met, he notes, the night he broke up with former wife Rachel Hunter.)  Stewart describes Lancaster as pretty much his biggest fan: She's known to stand in the first few rows at his concerts, rather than wait backstage, so she can see him in action and take a few pictures.  She has been kicked out a couple of times, Stewart notes, explaining that her height poses something of an issue for the audience members seated behind her. She's had her back scratched and all sorts of things. What we have to do now (is) give her a security guard whenever she goes in the audience.  And, yes, Lancaster is a big fan of Palm Beach as well: In fact, she shot an exercise video here when the couple was previously in town. That said, Stewart's history on the island goes back well before Lancaster -- to the early '90s.  We were in the middle of a winter tour. I was in New York. I was beginning to get a bit sick, and someone suggested going down to Palm Beach, he recalls. I stayed at the Ritz-Carlton and fell in love with that and started looking for a house.  There's just one problem with the island, Stewart hints: Even with his wealth, he has trouble keeping up with the Joneses.  I'm the smallest house on the beach! he says of his Palm Beach estate, which he purchased for $7 million. All the houses up the road, they're in the $50, $60 and $70 million (range).  Stewart has thought about starting fresh -- perhaps tearing down his manse and building another. But his five kids, particularly his three youngest ones -- Ruby, Renee and Liam -- have stopped him.  They said, 'We all grew up in this house. It's got far too many memories,'  Stewart concludes.  Charles Passy writes for The Palm Beach Post. , he believed,	 In The Lobotomist, Jack El-Hai's lively biography, Freeman comes across as a classic American type, a do-gooder and a go-getter with a bit of the huckster thrown in. Trained as a neurologist, he found a position at St. Elizabeth's, a large mental hospital in Washington, D.C., which, like most institutions then simply warehoused the mentally ill. Freeman was appalled at this waste of human potential. Convinced that mental illness stemmed from organic causes, he searched for a neurological solution and found it in a new procedure, developed by a Portuguese doctor and eventual Nobel laureate, Egas Moniz, who simply cut through neural pathways in the frontal lobes that, he believed, produced harmful or obsessive behavior. Freeman, who dismissed psychoanalysis as a sheer waste of time, jumped at Moniz's new procedure. Here was something tangible, something that an organicist like myself could understand and appreciate, he later wrote. A vision of the future unfolded. He formed a partnership with a skilled neurosurgeon, James Watts, and very quickly developed his own procedure, prefrontal lobotomy, which entailed drilling two holes in the skull, above the left and right frontal lobes, and then removing a dozen cores of white neural fibers. </P> 	nyt_eng_200501.gz

IRAQ (Baghdad) -- A Marine transport helicopter crashed in bad weather early Wednesday morning in the desert near the Jordanian border, killing 31 soldiers aboard. With the deaths of four other American soldiers elsewhere in Iraq, it was the deadliest single day for the United States since the invasion 22 months ago. By Dexter Filkins.  An early version has moved.  With NYT photos 1-3; 20-24; 36-37 and map and graphic.  IRAQ-BUSH (Washington) -- President Bush said Wednesday that the elections this weekend in Iraq mark a grand moment in Iraqi history that he described as part of a global march toward freedom. Yet he acknowledged that Iraqis themselves have not yet taken the initiative in defending their country and may doubt Washington's will to prevail against the insurgency. By David E. Sanger.  An early version has moved.  With NYT photos 5-7.  IRAQ-BUSH-MOOD (Washington) -- President Bush's opening statement at his news conference on Wednesday was striking for what it left out: any mention of the 31 Marines who died the night before in a helicopter crash in Iraq, the largest loss of American lives in a single incident since the war began. Bush instead focused on his long-term goal of ending tyranny in our world, and then cast the upcoming Iraqi election on Sunday, a critical test of his claim that the country is on the path to stability, as part of a march of freedom around the globe. The election was a grand moment in Iraqi history, the president said, adding that if had told the reporters in the room a few years before that the Iraqi people would be voting, you would look at me like some of you still look at me, with a kind of blank expression. By Elizabeth Bumiller.  With NYT photos 5-7.  CALIF-TRAINS (Glendale, Calif.) -- A commuter train smashed into an SUV left on the tracks by a suicidal man early Wednesday, sending the train off the rails in a wreck that killed 10 people and injured about 200, authorities said. Dozens of the injured were in critical condition. (Summary from wires.) By John N. Broder and Charlie LeDuff.  With photos NYT 28,29; 31-35; with graphic.  OBIT-JOHNSON (Undated) -- Philip Johnson, at once the elder statesman and the enfant terrible of American architecture, died Wednesday at the Glass House , the celebrated estate he built for himself in New Canaan, Conn., He was 98. By Paul Goldberger.  With photos NYT 4,8.  CHILE-PENSIONS (Santiago, Chile) -- Nearly 25 years ago, Chile embarked on a sweeping social experiment that has since been emulated in a score of other countries. Rather than finance pensions through a system to which workers, employers and the state all contributed, individual workers were asked to pay 10 percent of their salaries to private investment accounts under their own control. Under the Chilean program, which President Bush has cited as a model for his own plans to overhaul Social Security, the promise was that such investments would deliver monthly pension benefits larger than what the traditional system could possibly hope to offer. But now that the first generation of workers to depend on the new system is beginning to retire, Chile is finding that it is falling far short of what was originally advertised. By Larry Rohter. EXP-IRAQ (Baghdad) -- A Marine transport helicopter crashed in bad weather early Wednesday morning in the desert near the Jordanian border, killing 31 soldiers aboard. Yet he acknowledged that Iraqis themselves have not yet taken the initiative in defending their country and may doubt Washington's will to prevail against the insurgency. By David E. Sanger.  EXP-IRAQ-BUSH-MOOD (Washington) -- President Bush's opening statement at his news conference on Wednesday was striking for what it left out: any mention of the 31 Marines who died the night before in a helicopter crash in Iraq, the largest loss of American lives in a single incident since the war began. Bush instead focused on his long-term goal of ending tyranny in our world, and then cast the upcoming Iraqi election on Sunday, a critical test of his claim that the country is on the path to stability, as part of a march of freedom around the globe. EXP-CALIF-TRAINS (Glendale, Calif.) -- A commuter train smashed into an SUV left on the tracks by a suicidal man early Wednesday, sending the train off the rails in a wreck that killed 10 people and injured about 200, authorities said. EXP-CHILE-PENSIONS (Santiago, Chile) -- Nearly 25 years ago, Chile embarked on a sweeping social experiment that has since been emulated in a score of other countries. Rather than finance pensions through a system to which workers, employers and the state all contributed, individual workers were asked to pay 10 percent of their salaries to private investment accounts under their own control. The promise was that such investments would deliver monthly pension benefits larger than what the traditional system could possibly hope to offer. But now that the first generation of workers to depend on the new system is beginning to retire, Chile is finding that it is falling far short of what was originally advertised. By Larry Rohter.  EXP-TYCO-TRIAL (New York) -- Prosecutors in the fraud trial of the former top executives of Tyco International accused the two men Wednesday of systematically stealing millions of dollars in bonuses. The two defendants -- L. Dennis Kozlowski, the former chief executive, and Mark H. Swartz, the former finance chief -- are accused of stealing unauthorized bonuses and loans and of defrauding shareholders by selling stock they inflated by misrepresenting Tyco's financial condition. By Andrew Ross Sorkin. By Mark Landler.
