But Naqib, a 46-year-old dentist, will not vote on Sunday, when Iraqis will have their first opportunity in a generation to participate in an election with no predetermined outcome. It was, he said, far too dangerous when insurgent groups have warned that they will target anybody who approaches a polling station. Starkly put, Baghdad is not under control, either of the Iraqi interim government or the American military. By John F. Burns.  With photos NYT12-14 sent 1/21.  IRAQ-TERROR (Basra, Iraq) -- More than two-thirds of all Iraqis live in districts that have experienced insurgent attacks in the past month, according to an analysis of new intelligence data. More than half the Iraqis live in districts -- roughly the equivalent of large counties in the United States -- that suffered an average of at least one attack every three days in the 30 days ending Jan. 22, according to the analysis, which was conducted by The New York Times using Iraqi census data and information gathered by two private security companies with extensive access to intelligence on Iraq. By James Glanz and Thom Shanker.  With photo and graphic.  NATIONAL  (Coded a)  OBIT-JOHNSON (Undated) -- Philip Johnson, at once the elder statesman and the enfant terrible of American architecture, died Wednesday at the Glass House , the celebrated estate he built for himself in New Canaan, Conn., He was 98. By Paul Goldberger.  With photos NYT 4, 8.  FINANCIAL  (Coded f)  CHILE-PENSIONS (Santiago, Chile) -- Nearly 25 years ago, Chile embarked on a sweeping social experiment that has since been emulated in a score of other countries. Rather than finance pensions through a system to which workers, employers and the state all contributed, individual workers were asked to pay 10 percent of their salaries to private investment accounts under their own control. Under the Chilean program, which President Bush has cited as a model for his own plans to overhaul Social Security, the promise was that such investments would deliver monthly pension benefits larger than what the traditional system could possibly hope to offer. But now that the first generation of workers to depend on the new system is beginning to retire, Chile is finding that it is falling far short of what was originally advertised. By Larry Rohter.  UNINSURED (Undated) -- An attempt to extend health coverage to uninsured workers. By Milt Freudenheim.  With graphic.  STOCKS-MARKETPLACE (Undated) -- Profits this earnings season are better than expected so far, but have not been able to pull stocks out of a new year slump. Even though stocks have climbed the last two days, there are plenty of signs that the market may stay in the doldrums for a while. By Jonathan Fuerbringer.  HEALTHSOUTH-OUTLOOK (Undated) -- The HealthSouth sign was removed without fanfare from the Surgery Center of Des Moines shortly after federal investigators seized truckloads of financial records at the company's headquarters in Birmingham, Ala., two years ago. But the doctors at the Des Moines clinic are still partners with the big national health care chain, doing more cataract and plastic surgery procedures than ever, they say. With the help of doctors like the Iowa group and cash from patients around the country, HealthSouth has survived a business and public relations nightmare that began two years ago when the Justice Department and the Securities and Exchange Commission accused the company of accounting fraud totaling $2.6 billion. By Milt Freudenheim.  JOHNSON-APPRECIATION (Undated) -- An appreciation of Philip Johnson Johnson's career. By Nicolai Ouroussoff.  TV-SOROS-PROSECUTERS (Undated) -- We look at prosecutors' anger at a tide of anti-prosecution television programming financed by George Soros, pegged to tonight's broadcast of The Exonerated, the Bob Balaban play, on CourtTV. By Adam Liptak.  SUNDNACE-FILMS (Park City, Utah) -- A Critic's Notebook from the film festival. By Manohla Dargis.  TV-PBS-LESBIANS (Undated) -- Folo on the decision by PBS not to broadcast an episode, with lesbian characters, of a cartoon show, after criticism from new education secretary. But WGBH, the PBS station in Boston, will show it in March. By Julie Salamon.  PROJECT-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- The Manhattan Beach Project, by Peter Lefcourt (Simon &amp; Schuster), Reviewed by Janet Maslin.  CIRCUITS  (Coded a)  CIR-APPLE-HELP (San Francisco) -- It was just past noon on a recent weekday at the Apple store here, and the Genius Bar was buzzing. At one end of the 40-foot maple counter, a 25-year-old cherub-faced Genius in a black T-shirt spoke soothingly to a middle-aged customer whose iPod kept erasing songs. At the other end of the bar, another Genius, who appeared as amiable as he was knowledgeable, greeted a couple who had arrived with an ailing PowerBook.  In an age when human help of any kind is hard to come by, the eight or nine Geniuses -- Apple's term for its in-store technical support staff -- on duty at any given time here are a welcome anomaly. In fact, go to any of the 102 Apple-owned retail stores around the country and, if you are willing to wait, you will be treated to what is an increasingly rare service: free, face-to-face technical support. By Katie Hafner.  CIR-SCIENCE-FAIR (Undated) -- Technology is rapidly changing the world of science, but it is only now starting to change the world of the science project, a ritual of the academic year. Now that computers are second nature to many students, some teachers are abandoning the traditional cardboard displays in favor of electronic files. Some are even creating PowerPoint templates to make it easier for students to produce a smart-looking showcase. By Marcia Biederman.  CIR-CIRCUITS-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly column, on VHS-to-DVD recorders. By David Pogue.  CUR-ONLINE-SHOPPING (Undated) -- The weekly feature: It is high season for choosing a summer camp. By Michelle Slatalla.  CIR-DIGITAL-DIARY (Undated) -- The regular feature: Move over Mr. Potato Head -- here come anthropomorphic artichokes, avocados, and more; Mardi Gras site; Chinese New Year site. By Lisa Napoli.  CIR-VILLAGE-AID (Undated) -- The villagers of Cochiraya in southern Peru have been harvesting alpaca fleece since before the Incas arrived. But the fiber now generates meager returns on the local market, and most young people move in search of other work. Since July, however, a team volunteers has banded together to help Cochiraya sell its alpaca to the world. They have offered advice on how to set up and maintain an e-commerce site, arrange for international payments, draft a business plan and even choose a logo. Cochiraya is one of about 50 villages participating in a virtual aid network known as Nabuur, a Dutch nongovernmental organization that was begun in 2001. By Douglas Heingartner.  CIR-IPOD-DISCOS (Chicago) -- Whose iPod is this? yelled the manager. Is this the Pogues? Bar managers do not typically ask their clientele about the songs pumping out of the bar's music system. They tend to have intimate knowledge of the jukebox or leave any music questions to the DJ. But that is changing in some bars, where customers who bring their own iPods have started to take control of the tunes. By Ashlee Vance.  CIR-CELLPHONE-DJ (Undated) -- The cellphone already serves as a camera, a calendar and a portable arcade. But it could take on a new set of uses if a group of audio engineers, software designers and marketers has its way. The music-mixing software now on millions of PC's may soon become a fixture on cellphones and hand-held organizers. These early programs are crude, able to blend only a few sound snippets at a time. But by allowing amateur DJ's to create mixes with their cellphones, the software could offer musicians a new level of autonomy, and give nonmusicians another reason to stare at the little screens in their hands. By Noah Shachtman.  CIR-GEEK-NOTES (Undated) -- Techno news and notes:  -- From MediaGear, thumb-drive housings for camera flash memory cards;  -- Solio solar-powered battery charger for iPods, cellphones, PDA's, etc.;  -- DVD Jr. from Samsung, portable DVD-MP3 player for children;  -- Cellphone video game that allows up to 3 to play on a single phone;  -- Steadicam JR, a Hollywood-style accessory for home-movie makers;  -- LeapFrog pen computer;  -- GovTrack, a grad student's effort to make government transparent.  CIR-INTERNET-BROWSERS (Undated) -- The basics of browers: IT used to be that Internet Explorer, the one-size-fits-all Web browser bundled with every copy of Microsoft Windows, was enough for most people. It worked well and cost nothing. Who needed anything else? That attitude is fading these days as consumers begin to realize that other browsers offer more features, better security and greater freedom. Bells and whistles, perhaps, but some of them can be surprisingly useful. The number of competitors to Internet Explorer is surprisingly large and diverse. By Peter Wayner.  CIR-MICRO-DEVICES (Undated) -- What's Next: Bharat Bhushnan, at Ohio States, in seeking to design micro-scale devices, finds in the lotus leaf a secret to low friction and repelling water. The trick is not in smoothness but in the leaf's microscopic bumps. By Ian Austen.  Editors, there is no CIR-Q&amp;A this week.  HOME  (Coded l)  DESIGN-BLOGS (Undated) -- On a recent day, the cyber design world was buzzing. Bloggers at Apartmenttherapy.com had discovered the mystery manufacturer of a hot paint color called Fresh Melon Green. Over at treehugger.com, bamboo coffins were the talk of the moment. A post at Designsponge.com, meanwhile, praised ceramic bowls that double as wall sconces and endorsed all furniture on wheels. Now that blogs, or Web journals, influence just about everything from politics to buying gadgets, they are starting to transform the once clubby design community By Lockhart Steele.  ANTIQUE-GLASS-REPAIR (New York) -- Ted Foscolo was devastated when movers accidentally dropped a 19th-century glass chandelier he had bought for $16,000, destroying the fixture's blown glass shade with its engraved starburst pattern. I picked the chandelier for the beauty of the glass, said Foscolo, who tried to glue the fragments back together. Luckily, an Internet search led him to Michael Davis, a glass artist in Long Island City, who specializes in conjuring replicas of antique glass, sometimes armed only with shards. Equal parts detective, glass historian and technical wizard, Davis is among a handful of people in the country who have become specialized craftsmen in the field of reproducing glass masterpieces and beloved heirlooms. Part of the process, Davis explains, is deducing how the glass was created and channeling the glassmaker. By Andrea Truippin. The violence follows people to the market, to work and to school. It has become part of the public consciousness, surfacing even in television ads and newspaper cartoons. It is difficult but you get used to it, said Naba S. Hamid, a biology professor at Baghdad University. It has become part of our daily lives. Just like eating, sleeping, there is bombing. By Sabrina Tavernise.  RUSSIA-U.S. (Washinton) -- Shortly after the White House announced that President Bush would expand his trip to Moscow on Monday with stops to promote democracy in the former Soviet republics of Latvia and Georgia, the Russian foreign minister took the unusual step of sending a letter of protest to Secretary of State Condoleezza Rice. The letter has set the tone for a difficult presidential trip that has to balance attending a celebration in Red Square of the 60th anniversary of Nazi Germany's defeat without endorsing the subsequent Soviet occupation of Eastern Europe. As one administration official put it, If Bush gets out of this, he'll be Houdini. By Elisabeth Bumiller.  NATIONAL  (Coded a)  EARTH-BRIGHTNESS (Undated) -- Reversing a decades-long trend toward global dimming, the Earth's surface has become brighter in the last 15 years, scientists reported today. More sunlight -- and thus heat -- reaching the ground could help explain the record-high global temperatures reported in the 1990s and could accelerate the warming trend in the future. But the findings also highlight a major gap in the understanding of climate. Scientists do not know what caused the dimming and subsequent brightening or how these changes the rest of the climate system. By Kenneth Chang.  FINANCIAL  (Coded f)  HEALTHCARE-BURDEN (Undated) -- The relentless rise in health care costs is causing states and businesses to fight over whose job it is to insure workers. And nearly two dozen states, struggling with the growing burden of providing public assistance to people with jobs but no insurance, are looking to shift more of the financial burden onto the workers' employers. By Reed Abelson.  With photos NYT39,40.  IBM-EUROPE (Frankfurt, Germany) -- In the fierce global battle for jobs and capital, Western Europe just lost another round. IBM announced cuts of 10,000 to 13,000 jobs will fall heaviest on Germany, France, Britain and Italy, where the costs are high and growth prospects have recently been dim. IBM said Thursday that at least 60 percent of the cuts would be in its European operation. That division, which is based in Paris and also covers Africa and the Middle East, employs 100,000 people. By Mark Landler.  DECEPTION-NORRIS-COLUMN (Undated) -- To an extent not understood by investors or regulators, it appears that insurance companies, as well as investment and commercial banks, had profitable little businesses in what might politely be called financial statement beautification. They sold products that had little or no economic rationale, but that were promoted -- ever so quietly -- as being able to hide losses or smooth earnings. In some cases, the products arguably complied with accounting rules. But the intent was to deceive investors, and in the post-Enron world that is not permissible.  CONFERENCE-AD-COLUMN (Southampton, Bermuda -- Madison Avenue was warned Thursday that it risks being marginalized by profound changes in technology and demographics that are fundamentally changing the ways products are sold to consumers. By Stuart Elliott. LICENCE-PLATES-COLLECT (Undated) -- Lenny Shiller is a man who cares about historical accuracy. Each of the 50 antique cars he keeps is a testament to his preservationist beliefs -- down to his license plates, each of which is exactly as old as the vehicle that bears it. Such plates confer the final touch of authenticity to an old car. Typically, collectors find them at swap meets, at flea markets or on eBay. The plates may sell for just a few dollars, but using a year of manufacture tag can reveal a surprisingly complex world of legal regulations, automotive history and down-home Americana. By Tim Sultan.  TROPHY-GARDENS (Undated) -- In the Hamptons, where many people already have things like trophy wives and trophy houses, the trophy tree is turning out to be the latest must-have accoutrement. Hedges that screen out prying eyes are being pushed to ridiculous heights, hothouse orchids are trotted outdoors, and the price tags for glamour gardens and mock nature preserves are topping $1 million. And with just weeks to go before Memorial Day, the race to get your instant -- and established-looking -- garden in place is on. By Denny Lee  GOLF-FITNESS (Undated) -- It has been nearly a decade since Tiger Woods exploded onto the professional golf scene and began devouring his opponents with an aggressive, physical style of play. Today, the vision of the PGA Tour pro with his stomach straining at his Sansabelts has all but vanished, replaced by an army of flat-bellied young men with arms like cannons. It was just a matter of time before the golf fitness craze trickled down to the masses. Now, an increasing number of golfers send themselves off to boot camp to improve their games. By Joe Bargmann.  MEMPHIS-36-HOURS (Undated) -- What to do with 36 hours in Memphis, Tenn. By Robert Gordon. RETIRING-YOUNG (Los Angeles) -- For Rodney Rothman, 31, a former head writer for Late Show With David Letterman, 40 years was too long to wait for retirement. Burned out after years of writing jokes (well, a few years, anyway) and unemployed after the situation comedy he was working on was canceled, Rothman decided to hop off the workaday hamster wheel of life in Manhattan and Los Angeles and give retirement in Florida a try -- at age 28. Care of Roommate Finders, he found a room in the condominium of a widow with two cats and three birds at Century Village, a 775-acre, 16,000-resident retirement community in Boca Raton. From an intellectual property point of view, Rothman's six-month sojourn at Century Village may go down as one of the most productive retirements in history. He has turned his account of life there into a book called Early Bird (Simon &amp; Schuster) and a television pilot under development with NBC Universal; the book was published this week, and Rothman will find out soon whether NBC is going to pick up the TV show. By Warren St. John.  With photo.  RUNAWAY-BRIDES (Undated) -- In the annals of cold feet, there are celebrities like Jennifer Lopez, who thought better of making Ben Affleck husband No. 3 after planning an extravagant wedding. And now there is Jennifer Wilbanks, the runaway bride from Georgia, who set off a huge search-and-rescue by disappearing just days before her wedding day and pretending to have been kidnapped, only to reappear. While Wilbanks' fiance continues to profess his loyalty to her, people in the wedding industry say that in most cases of canceled nuptials, couples split up for good. They say, weddings are called off after a tearful breakup spurred by any number of reasons: infidelity, a last-minute push for a prenuptial agreement, a sense of panic brought on by out-of-control wedding preparations or, very often, the epiphany as the date approaches that this is not Mr. or Ms. Right after all. But the worst part, some say, is dealing with the emotions of a breakup that can be as tough as a divorce. By Mireya Navarro.  With photo.  MODERN-LOVE (Undated) -- I'm a good mother. This is not an idle boast; I have a signed certificate that says so. I earned this de facto mothering license by successfully completing four weeks of court-ordered parent classes. Why did a judge order me to do this? Was I a child abuser? Did I leave my children alone and go out to a bar? Was I on crack? No, I got a divorce. After 12 years of marriage and three children, ages 11, 7 and 4, my college sweetheart and I broke up. In the wake of our separation, I felt as if I might keel over and die from heartbreak. I rarely ate, slept or ventured out. Eventually, however, I began to pull myself together, thinking the worst was surely over. But I was wrong. Seven months into the separation I received a call from my lawyer. Are you sitting down? she asked. My husband, she explained, was seeking full custody of our children on the grounds that I was an unfit mother. By Katie Allison Granju. NIGER-URANIUM (Washington) -- A closer look at the early doubts expressed by the State Department about the reported sale of uranium from Niger to Iraq. The charge was included in President's Bush State of the Union speech in 2003, and played a part in the decision to invade Iraq. By Eric Lichtblau.  CHINA-EXPLORER (Beijing) -- A much-hyped map was unveiled Monday by a prominent Chinese lawyer and collector, claimed to be a 1763 copy of a map from 1418 proving that Zheng He, a 15th century Chinese explorer, circumnavigated the world long before the European explorers, and discovered America at least 75 years before Columbus. The claim was greeted skeptically by scholars but has generated considerable buzz. By Joseph Kahn.  BIRD-FLU-EUROPE (Rome) -- With outbreaks of bird flu continuing in Turkey, a top U.N. agricultural official recommended Monday that European countries should adopt policies like increased checks of airline passengers and their belongings to prevent the disease from spreading across the Continent. By Elisabeth Rosenthal.  MECCA-BRIDGE (Mina, Saudi Arabia) -- In the valley where the devil once roamed, this narrow stretch of holy land, nestled between mountains of basaltic rock, has long bedeviled the hajj, the pilgrimage that is a duty for devout Muslims. Here, they believe, the devil sought to tempt Abraham into disobeying God as he took his son to be slaughtered, only to be pelted with stones by the prophet. Each year, pilgrims pelt three columns at the site, known as Jamarat, to re-enact the moment in a symbolic rejection of temptation. But the site, where an imposing two-story overpass was built to carry the waves of pilgrims, has also been the scene of one catastrophe after another. Finally, on Sunday, bulldozers rolled in to change all that. The pedestrian bridge will be demolished to make way for a new four-story structure from which pilgrims can carry out the ritual. A Journal by Hassan M. Fattah.  NSA-LEGAL (Washington) -- Several defense lawyers, journalists, political and religious activists and other Americans with Middle East ties are expressing concerns that they might have been monitored under the National Security Agency's domestic surveillance program. And they are wondering whether those efforts were legal. By Eric Lichtblau.  NATIONAL GENERAL  (Will move in a news file.)  KING-BUSH (Washington) -- President Bush saluted the memory of the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. on Monday, saying that the slain civil rights leader, like Rosa Parks, who died last year, roused the dozing conscience of a complacent nation. By Richard W. Stevenson.  PLAYING-DEAD (Columbus, Ohio) -- Chuck Lamb might be the world's chattiest dead guy. He loves to talk, and his favorite topic is how he is fast becoming the most famous dead body alive. Isn't it incredible? said Lamb, who speaks at a volume that most people reserve for rock concerts. I had no idea it would blow up like this! Lamb became Dead Body Guy on Dec. 5. That is when he put up his Web site, deadbodyguy.com. The site features photos of Lamb playing dead in various scenes around his house. Crushed by his garage door. Electrocuted in the bathtub. One series shows Lamb face-down in a bowl of chicken soup, above a caption that reads Dying from bird flu. By staging his own death, Lamb hopes to attain a modest form of immortality. He says he always wanted to become a famous actor. Instead, at age 47 he finds himself with six children, working as a computer programmer for Nationwide Insurance. By Chris Maag.  With photo. IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- A double suicide bombing in Baghdad kills at least 15 people; an independent election monitoring commission says the Dec. 15 parliamentary vote was marred by some fraud but was basically fair. Plus other developments. By Robert F. Worth and Sabrina Tavernise.  An early lede has moved.  IRAQ-ITALY (Rome) -- Italy will withdraw its nearly 3,000 troops from Iraq by the year's end, the defense minister said Thursday amid a fiercely fought general election campaign in a nation where the Iraq war is not popular. This is not a retreat, a word that is not part of our vocabulary, the minister, Antonio Martino, told a parliamentary commission. Rather, he called it a dignified and just return. By Ian Fisher.  IRAN-SANCTIONS (Washington) -- A look at the options for sanctions against Iran over the nuclear issue. By Steven R. Weisman.  See also IRAN-OIL, below under Financial.  MIDEAST (Tel Aviv, Israel) -- A suicide bomber blows himself up in a crowded pedestrian mall in Tel Aviv, wounding at least 15. Islamic Jihad claims responsibility for the attack, the first in Israel in more than a month and less than a week until the Palestinian elections. By Dina Kraft.  An early lede has moved.  NEPAL-UNREST (New Delhi, India) -- In a replay of the raids that accompanied last February's palace coup, communication is cut across Nepal's capital, Katmandu, and nearly 100 political party activists are arrested on the eve of a planned political rally. By Somini Sengupta.  An early lede has moved.  BRITAIN-RENDITION (London) -- The British government may have permitted the use of its airspace and airports for American rendition flights more frequently than it has previously acknowledged, according to a leaked document published in a British weekly magazine. By Alan Cowell.  FRANCE-NUKES (Paris) -- President Jacques Chirac said Thursday that France will maintain its nuclear arsenal and doesn't rule out the possibility of using it if France is attacked. By Elaine Sciolino.  RUSSIA-GAS (Moscow) -- With Russia suffering from intense cold, the nation's gas supplies seem to be feeding Russia's big cities, to the occasional neglect of customers in the West. Gazprom, the Russia gas monopoly, denies this but buying nations say they are being shortchanged, less than a month after supplies were interrupted by a dispute with Ukraine. By Andrew Kramer.  See also RUSSIA-OIL-IPO below under Financial.  BRITAIN-TEACHER-CHECKS (London) -- Following feverish rhetoric in the news media and around Parliament about the employment of sex offenders in Britain's state-run schools, the government announces tightened background checks for teachers. Under the new proposals, anyone convicted or cautioned by the police for committing a sex offense involving children will be barred from teaching. By Sarah Lyall.  IVORY-COAST-UNREST (Abidjan, Ivory Coast) -- Developments in fourth day of political unrest in Ivory Coast, where the president is pushing for U.N. peacekeeping forces to leave. By Lydia Polgreen.  CIA-LEAK-RUSSERT (Undated) -- Recently unsealed court papers show that there is an additional dispute between Tim Russert and I. Lewis Libby, one concerning the ground rules on which they spoke. In the same papers, the special prosecutor in the case, Patrick J. Fitzgerald, accused Russert of selectivity in protecting confidential sources. The papers demonstrate that three of the main figures in Libby's trial have different understandings of how confidential source relationships are formed and protected, and they suggest that the trial may explore and even turn on how the craft of journalism is practiced in Washington. By Adam Liptak.  BUSH (Sterling, Va.) -- On the road with President Bush as he speaks on the economy. By Richard W. Stevenson.  WASHINGTON (w code)  HAITI-DEPORT (Washington) -- Dozens of lawyers around the country joined forces on Thursday to protest the continuing deportation of immigrants to Haiti, an island-nation dogged by worsening political instability, violence and deteriorating human rights conditions. The State Department has warned Americans against traveling to Haiti because of the continuing instability. And in what amounted to a mass legal protest, the lawyers filed motions in dozens of individual deportation cases, calling on immigration judges to stop the deportations, saying that the lives of their clients were in danger. By Rachel Swarns.  ALITO-CONFIRM (Washington) -- In an indication of how Judge Samuel A. Alito Jr. may fare when the Senate Judiciary Committee votes on his confirmation next Tuesday, Sen. Patrick J. Leahy of Vermont, the ranking Democrat on the panel, declared his intention to vote against him. I cannot vote for this nomination, Leahy said. I will not vote for this nomination. Leahy said he was disturbed by Alito's responses during the committee hearings last week on the issues of privacy and conflicts between government and individuals. He also said he believed Alito, who has spent his entire legal career working for the government, would be too deferential to what the senator called his patrons. By David D. Kirkpatrick.  FINANCIAL (f code)  QWEST-NACCHIO-TRIAL (Undated) -- A look at the coming trial in Denver of Qwest's Joseph P. Nacchio, charged with 42 counts of insider trading The decision to pursue one of the last titans of the Internet bubble on insider trading charges may be sign that prosecutors are simplifying their cases and steering clear of complex cases that rely on juries grasping esoteric financial data and efforts to manipulate it. By Ken Belson.  IRAN-OIL (Undated) -- While the international community debates how to deal with Iran's nuclear program, it is the country's oil that is causing concerns to energy analysts these days. Over the weekend, Iran's president, Mahmoud Ahmadinejad, hinted that his country might be willing to use its oil weapon -- meaning it would be willing to curb its oil exports -- if faced with international sanctions. At the same time, some members of the U.S. Senate said Iran should be sanctioned regardless of what that means for oil prices. Such talk is causing waves of anxiety in the oil markets, which have been rattled by Iran's decision to resume its nuclear research at the beginning of the year. By Jad Mouawad.  NORRIS-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly column by Floyd Norris.  MERRILL-EARNINGS (Undated) -- Merrill Lynch said Thursday that fourth-quarter profit rose 25 percent as stock-trading revenue and merger advisory fees surged. By Jenny Anderson.  JAPAN-STOCKS (Tokyo) -- Tokyo Stock Exchange executives announced plans to double their computers' capacity for executing and clearing trades by the end of this year, a day after the exchange nearly collapsed under a barrage of electronic orders.  An early lede, on the Thursday rebound in stock prices, has moved.  INDIA-AIRLINES (Bangalore, India) -- India's largest airline, Jet Airways, said it would buy rival Air Sahara for $500 million in cash in the first signs of consolidation in an increasingly competitive airline market. Jet Airways said the combined market share would give Jet Airways half of the domestic market. By Saritha Rai.  DISNEY-PIXAR (Undated) -- Disney is in talks with Pixar that could result in one of three deals: an outright acquisition, a purchase of a stake or a straight-up distribution deal. No transaction is imminent, but Disney is particularly interested in a deal that would gives its parks the rights to Pixar characters. By Laura M. Holson.  RUSSIA-OIL-IPO (London) -- London will be home to what may be one of the largest initial public offerings ever later this year, when the Russian oil giant Rosneft makes its debut on the London Stock Exchange. The listing of Rosneft, expected in June, is one of the most visible rewards yet for London's financial markets, which have embraced the Russian and central Asian companies buoyed by the recent energy and commodities boom. But the rash of IPO's is prompting concerns that London's competitive advantage is thanks in part to less stringent listing requirements than the United States. By Heather Timmons.  HEDGE-RETURNS (Undated) -- The weekly Insider column: The conventional wisdom holds that too much money is pouring into hedge funds and other alternative investments and that returns will diminish as result. Not so fast, says a new study. By Jenny Anderson. Burke then told Ainge, who knew nothing of the situation.
